<p id="mwDA"><b id="mwDQ">Fish</b> are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal" id="mwDg">aquatic</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Craniate" title="Craniate" id="mwDw">craniate</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gill" title="Gill" id="mwEA">gill</a>-bearing animals that lack <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)" id="mwEQ">limbs</a> with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Digit_(anatomy)" title="Digit (anatomy)" id="mwEg">digits</a>. Included in this definition are the living <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hagfish" title="Hagfish" id="mwEw">hagfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwFA">lampreys</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes" id="mwFQ">cartilaginous</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bony_fish" title="Bony fish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwFg">bony fish</a> as well as various extinct related groups. Approximately 95% of living fish species are ray-finned fish, belonging to the class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii" id="mwFw">Actinopterygii</a>, with around 99% of those being <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Teleost" title="Teleost" id="mwGA">teleosts</a>.</p>

<p id="mwGQ">The earliest organisms that can be classified as fish were soft-bodied <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chordate" title="Chordate" id="mwGg">chordates</a> that first appeared during the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cambrian" title="Cambrian" id="mwGw">Cambrian</a> period. Although they lacked a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate" id="mwHA">true spine</a>, they possessed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Notochord" title="Notochord" id="mwHQ">notochords</a> which allowed them to be more agile than their invertebrate counterparts. Fish would continue to evolve through the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic" id="mwHg">Paleozoic</a> era, diversifying into a wide variety of forms. Many fish of the Paleozoic developed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placodermi" title="Placodermi" id="mwHw">external armor</a> that protected them from predators. The first fish with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jaw" title="Jaw" id="mwIA">jaws</a> appeared in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Silurian" title="Silurian" id="mwIQ">Silurian</a> period, after which many (such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shark" title="Shark" id="mwIg">sharks</a>) became formidable marine predators rather than just the prey of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Arthropod" title="Arthropod" id="mwIw">arthropods</a>.</p>

<p id="mwJA">Most fish are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm" id="mwJQ">ectothermic</a> ("cold-blooded"), allowing their body temperatures to vary as ambient temperatures change, though some of the large active swimmers like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./White_shark" title="White shark" class="mw-redirect" id="mwJg">white shark</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tuna" title="Tuna" id="mwJw">tuna</a> can hold a higher <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Core_temperature" title="Core temperature" class="mw-redirect" id="mwKA">core temperature</a>.<sup about="#mwt26" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-1"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mwKQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwKg">[1]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt30" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-2"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-2" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 2;" id="mwKw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwLA">[2]</span></a></sup> Fish can acoustically communicate with each other, most often in the context of feeding, aggression or courtship.<sup about="#mwt34" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Bioacoustics_3-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Bioacoustics"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Bioacoustics-3"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Bioacoustics-3" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 3;" id="mwLQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwLg">[3]</span></a></sup></p> 

<p id="mwLw">Fish are abundant in most bodies of water. They can be found in nearly all aquatic environments, from high mountain streams (e.g., <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Salvelinus" title="Salvelinus" id="mwMA">char</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gudgeon_(fish)" title="Gudgeon (fish)" id="mwMQ">gudgeon</a>) to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Abyssal_zone" title="Abyssal zone" id="mwMg">abyssal</a> and even <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hadal_zone" title="Hadal zone" id="mwMw">hadal</a> depths of the deepest oceans (e.g., <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cusk-eels" title="Cusk-eels" class="mw-redirect" id="mwNA">cusk-eels</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Snailfish" title="Snailfish" id="mwNQ">snailfish</a>), although no species has yet been documented in the deepest 25% of the ocean.<sup about="#mwt40" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-4"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-4" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 4;" id="mwNg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwNw">[4]</span></a></sup> With 34,300 described species, fish exhibit greater species diversity than any other group of vertebrates.<sup about="#mwt44" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-5"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-5" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 5;" id="mwOA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwOQ">[5]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwOg">Fish are an important resource for humans worldwide, especially <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_(food)" title="Fish (food)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwOw">as food</a>. Commercial and subsistence fishers hunt fish in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wild_fisheries" title="Wild fisheries" id="mwPA">wild fisheries</a> or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_farm" title="Fish farm" class="mw-redirect" id="mwPQ">farm</a> them in ponds or in cages in the ocean (in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture" id="mwPg">aquaculture</a>). They are also caught by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Recreational_fishing" title="Recreational fishing" id="mwPw">recreational fishers</a>, kept as pets, raised by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fishkeeping" title="Fishkeeping" id="mwQA">fishkeepers</a>, and exhibited in public <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aquarium" title="Aquarium" id="mwQQ">aquaria</a>. Fish have had a role in culture through the ages, serving as deities, religious symbols, and as the subjects of art, books and movies.</p>

<p id="mwQg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod" id="mwQw">Tetrapods</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amphibian" title="Amphibian" id="mwRA">amphibians</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reptile" title="Reptile" id="mwRQ">reptiles</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bird" title="Bird" id="mwRg">birds</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammal" title="Mammal" id="mwRw">mammals</a>) emerged within <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii" id="mwSA">lobe-finned fishes</a>, so <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cladistically" title="Cladistically" class="mw-redirect" id="mwSQ">cladistically</a> they are fish as well. However, traditionally fish (<b id="mwSg">pisces</b> or <b id="mwSw">ichthyes</b>) are rendered <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwTA">paraphyletic</a> by excluding the tetrapods, and are therefore not considered a formal taxonomic grouping in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Systematics" title="Systematics" id="mwTQ">systematic biology</a>, unless it is used in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cladistic" title="Cladistic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwTg">cladistic</a> sense, including tetrapods,<sup about="#mwt50" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-6" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-6"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-6" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 6;" id="mwTw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwUA">[6]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt54" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-7" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-7"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwUQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwUg">[7]</span></a></sup> although usually "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate" id="mwUw">vertebrate</a>" is preferred and used for this purpose (fish plus tetrapods) instead. Furthermore, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cetaceans" title="Cetaceans" class="mw-redirect" id="mwVA">cetaceans</a>, although mammals, have often been considered fish by various cultures and time periods.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="1" id="mwVQ"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2>
<p id="mwVg">The word for <i id="mwVw">fish</i> in English and the other <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages" id="mwWA">Germanic languages</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./German_language" title="German language" id="mwWQ">German</a> <span title="German-language text" about="#mwt59" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"lang","href":"./Template:Lang"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"de"},"2":{"wt":"Fisch"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwWg"><i lang="de">Fisch</i></span><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_containing_German-language_text" about="#mwt59" id="mwWw"/>; <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gothic_language" title="Gothic language" id="mwXA">Gothic</a> <span title="Gothic-language text" about="#mwt60" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"lang","href":"./Template:Lang"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"got"},"2":{"wt":"fisks"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwXQ"><i lang="got">fisks</i></span><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_containing_Gothic-language_text" about="#mwt60" id="mwXg"/>) is inherited from <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language" id="mwXw">Proto-Germanic</a>, and is related to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Latin_language" title="Latin language" class="mw-redirect" id="mwYA">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text" about="#mwt61" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"lang","href":"./Template:Lang"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"la"},"2":{"wt":"piscis"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwYQ"><i lang="la">piscis</i></span><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_containing_Latin-language_text" about="#mwt61" id="mwYg"/> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Old_Irish_language" title="Old Irish language" class="mw-redirect" id="mwYw">Old Irish</a> <span title="Old Irish (to 900)-language text" about="#mwt62" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"lang","href":"./Template:Lang"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"sga"},"2":{"wt":"īasc"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwZA"><i lang="sga">īasc</i></span><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_containing_Old_Irish_(to_900)-language_text" about="#mwt62" id="mwZQ"/>, though the exact root is unknown; some authorities reconstruct a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language" id="mwZg">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <span title="Proto-Indo-European-language text" about="#mwt63" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"lang","href":"./Template:Lang"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"ine-x-proto"},"2":{"wt":"*peysk-"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwZw"><span typeof="mw:Entity">*</span><i lang="ine">peysk-</i></span><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_containing_Proto-Indo-European-language_text" about="#mwt63" id="mwaA"/>, attested only in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Italic_languages" title="Italic languages" id="mwaQ">Italic</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages" id="mwag">Celtic</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages" id="mwaw">Germanic</a>.<sup about="#mwt67" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-8" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-8"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwbA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwbQ">[8]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt68" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-9" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-9"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-9" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 9;" id="mwbg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwbw">[9]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt72" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-10" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-10"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mwcA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwcQ">[10]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt73" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-11" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-11"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-11" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 11;" id="mwcg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwcw">[11]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwdA">The English word once had a much broader usage than its current biological meaning. Names such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Starfish" title="Starfish" id="mwdQ">starfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish" id="mwdg">jellyfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shellfish" title="Shellfish" id="mwdw">shellfish</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish" id="mweA">cuttlefish</a> attest to almost any fully aquatic animal (including whales) once being <i id="mweQ">fish</i>. "Correcting" such names (e.g. to <i id="mweg">sea star</i>) is an attempt to retroactively apply the current meaning of <i id="mwew">fish</i> to words that were coined when it had a different meaning.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt74" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"cn","href":"./Template:Cn"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"January 2023"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwfA"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_January_2023" about="#mwt74"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt74" id="mwfQ"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="2" id="mwfg"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt76" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Evolution of fish"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwfw"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt76" id="mwgA">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Evolution_of_fish" title="Evolution of fish">Evolution of fish</a></div><p id="mwgQ">Fish, as vertebrata, developed as sister of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tunicata" title="Tunicata" class="mw-redirect" id="mwgg">tunicata</a>. As the tetrapods emerged deep within the fishes group, as sister of the lungfish, characteristics of fish are typically shared by tetrapods, including having vertebrae and a cranium.</p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb mw:ExpandedAttrs" about="#mwt84" data-mw='{"attribs":[["alt",{"html":"alt=Drawing of animal with large mouth, long tail, very small dorsal fins, and <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwiw"><a href="./File:Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwjA"><img resource="./File:Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg/220px-Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="5271" data-file-height="3203" data-file-type="bitmap" height="134" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg/330px-Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg/440px-Eastmanosteus_pustulosus.jpg 2x" id="mwjQ"/></a><figcaption id="mwjg">Lower jaw of the placoderm <i id="mwjw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Eastmanosteus" title="Eastmanosteus" id="mwkA">Eastmanosteus</a> pustulosus</i>, showing the shearing structures ("teeth") on its oral surface; from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Devonian" title="Devonian" id="mwkQ">Devonian</a> of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin" id="mwkg">Wisconsin</a></figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwkw">Early fish from the fossil record are represented by a group of small, jawless, armored fish known as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ostracoderm" title="Ostracoderm" id="mwlA">ostracoderms</a>. Jawless fish lineages are mostly extinct. An extant clade, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwlQ">lampreys</a> may approximate ancient pre-jawed fish. The first jaws are found in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placodermi" title="Placodermi" id="mwlg">Placodermi</a> fossils. They lacked distinct teeth, having instead the oral surfaces of their jaw plates modified to serve the various purposes of teeth. The diversity of jawed vertebrates may indicate the evolutionary advantage of a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_jaw" title="Fish jaw" id="mwlw">jawed mouth</a>. It is unclear if the advantage of a hinged jaw is greater biting force, improved respiration, or a combination of factors.</p>

<p id="mwmA">Fish may have evolved from a creature similar to a coral-like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sea_squirt" title="Sea squirt" class="mw-redirect" id="mwmQ">sea squirt</a>, whose larvae resemble primitive fish in important ways. The first ancestors of fish may have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neoteny" title="Neoteny" id="mwmg">kept the larval form into adulthood</a> (as some sea squirts do today).</p>

<section data-mw-section-id="3" id="mwmw"><h3 id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</h3>
<p id="mwnA">Fishes are a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly" id="mwnQ">paraphyletic</a> group: that is, any <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Clade" title="Clade" id="mwng">clade</a> containing all fish also contains the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod" id="mwnw">tetrapods</a>, which are not fish (though they include fish-shaped forms, such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cetacea" title="Cetacea" id="mwoA">Whales and Dolphins</a> or the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Extinct" title="Extinct" class="mw-redirect" id="mwoQ">extinct</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ichthyopterygia" title="Ichthyopterygia" id="mwog">ichthyosaurs</a>, which acquired a fish-like body shape due to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Secondary_aquatic_adaptation" title="Secondary aquatic adaptation" class="mw-redirect" id="mwow">secondary aquatic adaptation</a>, see <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Evolution_of_cetaceans" title="Evolution of cetaceans" id="mwpA">evolution of cetaceans</a>). </p>

<p id="mwpQ">The following <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cladogram" title="Cladogram" id="mwpg">cladogram</a> shows <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Clade" title="Clade" id="mwpw">clades</a> - some with, some without <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Extant_taxa" title="Extant taxa" class="mw-redirect" id="mwqA">extant</a> relatives - that are traditionally considered as "fishes" (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cyan" title="Cyan" id="mwqQ">cyan</a> line) and the tetrapods (four-limbed vertebrates), which are mostly terrestrial. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Extinct" title="Extinct" class="mw-redirect" id="mwqg">Extinct</a> groups are marked with a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dagger_(mark)" title="Dagger (mark)" id="mwqw">dagger</a> (†).</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="4" id="mwsQ"><h3 id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwsg"><a href="./File:Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwsw"><img resource="./File:Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg/220px-Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1441" data-file-type="bitmap" height="106" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg/330px-Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg/440px-Leedsichthys_problematicus.jpg 2x" id="mwtA"/></a><figcaption id="mwtQ"><i id="mwtg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leedsichthys" title="Leedsichthys" id="mwtw">Leedsichthys</a>,</i> of the subclass Actinopterygii, is the largest known fish, with estimates in 2005 putting its maximum size at <span about="#mwt176" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"16"},"2":{"wt":"m"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwuA">16 metres (52</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt176"> </span><span about="#mwt176">ft)</span>.</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwuQ">Fishes are a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwug">paraphyletic</a> group and for this reason, groups such as the class <i id="mwuw">Pisces</i> seen in older reference works are no longer used in formal classifications. Traditional classification divides fish into three <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon" class="mw-redirect" id="mwvA">extant</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)" id="mwvQ">classes</a>, and with extinct forms sometimes classified within the tree, sometimes as their own classes:<sup about="#mwt179" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_15-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Romer, A 1977"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-15"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 15;" id="mwvg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwvw">[15]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt180" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-16" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-16"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-16" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 16;" id="mwwA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwwQ">[16]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul id="mwwg"><li id="mwww">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Agnatha" title="Agnatha" id="mwxA">Agnatha</a> (jawless fish)
<ul id="mwxQ"><li id="mwxg">Subclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cyclostomata" title="Cyclostomata" class="mw-redirect" id="mwxw">Cyclostomata</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hagfish" title="Hagfish" id="mwyA">hagfish</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwyQ">lampreys</a>)</li>
<li id="mwyg">Subclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ostracodermi" title="Ostracodermi" class="mw-redirect" id="mwyw">Ostracodermi</a> (armoured jawless fish) †</li></ul></li>
<li id="mwzA">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes" id="mwzQ">Chondrichthyes</a> (cartilaginous fish)
<ul id="mwzg"><li id="mwzw">Subclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Elasmobranchii" title="Elasmobranchii" id="mw0A">Elasmobranchii</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shark" title="Shark" id="mw0Q">sharks</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Batoidea" title="Batoidea" id="mw0g">rays</a>)</li>
<li id="mw0w">Subclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Holocephali" title="Holocephali" id="mw1A">Holocephali</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chimaera" title="Chimaera" id="mw1Q">chimaeras</a> and extinct relatives)</li></ul></li>
<li id="mw1g">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placodermi" title="Placodermi" id="mw1w">Placodermi</a> (armoured fish) †</li>
<li id="mw2A">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Acanthodii" title="Acanthodii" id="mw2Q">Acanthodii</a> ("spiny sharks", sometimes classified under Actinopterygii)</li>
<li id="mw2g">Superclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes" id="mw2w">Osteichthyes</a> (bony fish)
<ul id="mw3A"><li id="mw3Q">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii" id="mw3g">Actinopterygii</a> (ray finned fishes)</li>
<li id="mw3w">Clade <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii" id="mw4A">Sarcopterygii</a> (lobe finned fishes, ancestors of tetrapods)</li></ul></li></ul>

<p id="mw4Q">The above scheme is the one most commonly encountered in non-specialist and general works. Many of the above groups are paraphyletic, in that they have given rise to successive groups: Agnathans are ancestral to Chondrichthyes, who again have given rise to Acanthodiians, the ancestors of Osteichthyes. With the arrival of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature" id="mw4g">phylogenetic nomenclature</a>, the fishes has been split up into a more detailed scheme, with the following major groups:</p>
<ul id="mw4w"><li id="mw5A">Class Myxini (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hagfish" title="Hagfish" id="mw5Q">hagfish</a>)</li>
<li id="mw5g">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pteraspidomorphi" title="Pteraspidomorphi" id="mw5w">Pteraspidomorphi</a> † (early jawless fish)</li>
<li id="mw6A">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thelodonti" title="Thelodonti" id="mw6Q">Thelodonti</a> †</li>
<li id="mw6g">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anaspida" title="Anaspida" id="mw6w">Anaspida</a> †</li>
<li id="mw7A">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hyperoartia" title="Hyperoartia" id="mw7Q">Petromyzontida or Hyperoartia</a>
<ul id="mw7g"><li id="mw7w">Petromyzontidae (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mw8A">lampreys</a>)</li></ul></li>
<li id="mw8Q">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Conodont" title="Conodont" id="mw8g">Conodonta</a> (conodonts) †</li>
<li id="mw8w">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cephalaspidomorphi" title="Cephalaspidomorphi" id="mw9A">Cephalaspidomorphi</a> † (early jawless fish)
<ul id="mw9Q"><li id="mw9g">(unranked) <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Galeaspida" title="Galeaspida" id="mw9w">Galeaspida</a> †</li>
<li id="mw-A">(unranked) <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pituriaspida" title="Pituriaspida" id="mw-Q">Pituriaspida</a> †</li>
<li id="mw-g">(unranked) <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osteostraci" title="Osteostraci" id="mw-w">Osteostraci</a> †</li></ul></li>
<li id="mw_A">Infraphylum <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata" id="mw_Q">Gnathostomata</a> (jawed vertebrates)
<ul id="mw_g"><li id="mw_w">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placodermi" title="Placodermi" id="mwAQA">Placodermi</a> † (armoured fish)</li>
<li id="mwAQE">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes" id="mwAQI">Chondrichthyes</a> (cartilaginous fish)</li>
<li id="mwAQM">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Acanthodii" title="Acanthodii" id="mwAQQ">Acanthodii</a> † (spiny sharks)</li>
<li id="mwAQU">Superclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes" id="mwAQY">Osteichthyes</a> (bony fish)
<ul id="mwAQc"><li id="mwAQg">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii" id="mwAQk">Actinopterygii</a> (ray-finned fish)
<ul id="mwAQo"><li id="mwAQs">Subclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chondrostei" title="Chondrostei" id="mwAQw">Chondrostei</a>
<ul id="mwAQ0"><li id="mwAQ4">Order <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Acipenseriformes" title="Acipenseriformes" id="mwAQ8">Acipenseriformes</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon" id="mwARA">sturgeons</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paddlefish" title="Paddlefish" id="mwARE">paddlefishes</a>)</li>
<li id="mwARI">Order <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Polypteriformes" title="Polypteriformes" class="mw-redirect" id="mwARM">Polypteriformes</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reedfish" title="Reedfish" id="mwARQ">reedfishes</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bichir" title="Bichir" id="mwARU">bichirs</a>).</li></ul></li>
<li id="mwARY">Subclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neopterygii" title="Neopterygii" id="mwARc">Neopterygii</a>
<ul id="mwARg"><li id="mwARk">Infraclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Holostei" title="Holostei" id="mwARo">Holostei</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gar" title="Gar" id="mwARs">gars</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bowfin" title="Bowfin" id="mwARw">bowfins</a>)</li>
<li id="mwAR0">Infraclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Teleostei" title="Teleostei" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAR4">Teleostei</a> (many orders of common fish)</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li id="mwAR8">Class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii" id="mwASA">Sarcopterygii</a> (lobe-finned fish)
<ul id="mwASE"><li id="mwASI">Subclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Actinistia" title="Actinistia" class="mw-redirect" id="mwASM">Actinistia</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth" id="mwASQ">coelacanths</a>)</li>
<li id="mwASU">Subclass Dipnoi (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lungfish" title="Lungfish" id="mwASY">lungfish</a>, sister group to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapods" title="Tetrapods" class="mw-redirect" id="mwASc">tetrapods</a>)</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>

<p id="mwASg">† – indicates extinct taxon<br id="mwASk"/>Some palaeontologists contend that because <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Conodonta" title="Conodonta" class="mw-redirect" id="mwASo">Conodonta</a> are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chordate" title="Chordate" id="mwASs">chordates</a>, they are primitive fish. For a fuller treatment of this taxonomy, see the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate" id="mwASw">vertebrate</a> article.</p>

<p id="mwAS0">The position of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hagfish" title="Hagfish" id="mwAS4">hagfish</a> in the phylum Chordata is not settled. Phylogenetic research in 1998 and 1999 supported the idea that the hagfish and the lampreys form a natural group, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cyclostomata" title="Cyclostomata" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAS8">Cyclostomata</a>, that is a sister group of the Gnathostomata.<sup about="#mwt183" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-17" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-17"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-17" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 17;" id="mwATA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATE">[17]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt184" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-18" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-18"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-18" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 18;" id="mwATI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATM">[18]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwATQ">The various fish groups account for more than half of vertebrate species. As of 2006,<sup about="#mwt186" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20064–5_19-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Nelson"},"2":{"wt":"2006"},"pp":{"wt":"4–5"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20064–5-19" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mwATU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATY">[19]</span></a></sup> there are almost 28,000 known <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon" class="mw-redirect" id="mwATc">extant</a> species, of which almost 27,000 are bony fish, with 970 <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes" id="mwATg">sharks, rays, and chimeras</a> and about 108 hagfish and lampreys. A third of these species fall within the nine largest families; from largest to smallest, these families are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cyprinidae" title="Cyprinidae" id="mwATk">Cyprinidae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gobiidae" title="Gobiidae" id="mwATo">Gobiidae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cichlidae" title="Cichlidae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwATs">Cichlidae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Characidae" title="Characidae" id="mwATw">Characidae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Loricariidae" title="Loricariidae" id="mwAT0">Loricariidae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Balitoridae" title="Balitoridae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAT4">Balitoridae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Serranidae" title="Serranidae" id="mwAT8">Serranidae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Labridae" title="Labridae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUA">Labridae</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scorpaenidae" title="Scorpaenidae" id="mwAUE">Scorpaenidae</a>. About 64 families are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Monotypic" title="Monotypic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUI">monotypic</a>, containing only one species. The final total of extant species may grow to exceed 32,500.<sup about="#mwt189" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20063_20-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Nelson"},"2":{"wt":"2006"},"p":{"wt":"3"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20063-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 20;" id="mwAUM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAUQ">[20]</span></a></sup> Each year, new <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Species" title="Species" id="mwAUU">species</a> are discovered and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Taxonomy" title="Taxonomy" id="mwAUY">scientifically described</a>. As of 2016,<sup about="#mwt195" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto_21-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-auto-21"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-auto-21" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 21;" id="mwAUc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAUg">[21]</span></a></sup> there are over 32,000 documented species of bony fish and over 1,100 species of cartilaginous fish. Species are lost through <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Extinction" title="Extinction" id="mwAUk">extinction</a> (see <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Biodiversity_crisis" title="Biodiversity crisis" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUo">biodiversity crisis</a>). Recent examples are the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Psephurus" title="Psephurus" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUs">Chinese paddlefish</a> or the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Smooth_handfish" title="Smooth handfish" id="mwAUw">smooth handfish</a>.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="5" id="mwAU0"><h3 id="Diversity">Diversity</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt196" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Diversity of fish"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAU4"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt196" id="mwAU8">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diversity_of_fish" title="Diversity of fish">Diversity of fish</a></div>

<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" title="Examples" typeof="mw:Extension/gallery" about="#mwt200" data-mw='{"name":"gallery","attrs":{"mode":"packed","title":"Examples","of":"","the":"","major":"","classes":"","fish":""},"body":{"extsrc":"\nPacific_hagfish_Myxine.jpg|Agnatha&lt;br> ([[Pacific hagfish]])\nHornhai (Heterodontus francisci).JPG|Chondrichthyes &lt;br>([[Horn shark]])\nSalmo trutta.jpg|Actinopterygii&lt;br> ([[Brown trout]])\nLatimeria chalumnae01.jpg|Sarcopterygii &lt;br>([[Coelacanth]])\n"}}' id="mwAVA">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 98px;" id="mwAWE"><div class="thumb" style="width: 96px;" id="mwAWI"><span typeof="mw:File" id="mwAWM"><a href="./File:Salmo_trutta.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Actinopterygii (Brown trout)" id="mwAWQ"><img alt="Actinopterygii (Brown trout)" resource="./File:Salmo_trutta.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Salmo_trutta.jpg/144px-Salmo_trutta.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3000" data-file-type="bitmap" height="120" width="96" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Salmo_trutta.jpg/216px-Salmo_trutta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Salmo_trutta.jpg/288px-Salmo_trutta.jpg 2x" id="mwAWU"/></a></span></div><div class="gallerytext" id="mwAWY">Actinopterygii<br id="mwAWc"/> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Brown_trout" title="Brown trout" id="mwAWg">Brown trout</a>)</div></li>

</ul>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAXE"><a href="./File:Leafydragon.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAXI"><img alt="Photo of fish with many narrow, straight appendages. Some are end in points, and others are longer, ending in two or three approximately flat, triangular flaps, each with a dark spot." resource="./File:Leafydragon.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leafydragon.jpg/220px-Leafydragon.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="572" data-file-height="420" data-file-type="bitmap" height="162" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leafydragon.jpg/330px-Leafydragon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leafydragon.jpg/440px-Leafydragon.jpg 2x" id="mwAXM"/></a><figcaption id="mwAXQ">A relative of the seahorses, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leafy_seadragon" title="Leafy seadragon" id="mwAXU">leafy seadragon</a>'s appendages allow it to camouflage (in the form of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crypsis" title="Crypsis" id="mwAXY">crypsis</a>) with the surrounding <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Seaweed" title="Seaweed" id="mwAXc">seaweed</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAXg"><a href="./File:Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAXk"><img resource="./File:Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png/220px-Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="729" data-file-height="531" data-file-type="bitmap" height="160" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png/330px-Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png/440px-Synchiropus_splendidus_2_Luc_Viatour_cropped.png 2x" id="mwAXo"/></a><figcaption id="mwAXs"> The psychedelic <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mandarin_dragonet" title="Mandarin dragonet" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAXw">mandarin dragonet</a> is one of only two fish species where the blue colouring has been shown to be due to blue pigment containing <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chromatophores" title="Chromatophores" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAX0">chromatophores</a> in the skin.<sup about="#mwt205" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-22" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-22"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-22" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 22;" id="mwAX4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAX8">[22]</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwAYA">The term "fish" most precisely describes any non-<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod" id="mwAYE">tetrapod</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Craniate" title="Craniate" id="mwAYI">craniate</a> (i.e. an animal with a skull and in most cases a backbone) that has <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gill" title="Gill" id="mwAYM">gills</a> throughout life and whose limbs, if any, are in the shape of fins.<sup about="#mwt207" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson20062_23-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Nelson"},"2":{"wt":"2006"},"p":{"wt":"2"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson20062-23" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 23;" id="mwAYQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYU">[23]</span></a></sup> Unlike groupings such as birds or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammal" title="Mammal" id="mwAYY">mammals</a>, fish are not a single <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Clade" title="Clade" id="mwAYc">clade</a> but a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAYg">paraphyletic</a> collection of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Taxon" title="Taxon" id="mwAYk">taxa</a>, including <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hagfish" title="Hagfish" id="mwAYo">hagfishes</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwAYs">lampreys</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes" id="mwAYw">sharks and rays</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii" id="mwAY0">ray-finned fish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth" id="mwAY4">coelacanths</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lungfish" title="Lungfish" id="mwAY8">lungfish</a>.<sup about="#mwt210" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973_24-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"p":{"wt":"3"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973-24" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 24;" id="mwAZA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZE">[24]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt214" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-25" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-25"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-25" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 25;" id="mwAZI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZM">[25]</span></a></sup> Indeed, lungfish and coelacanths are closer relatives of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod" id="mwAZQ">tetrapods</a> (such as mammals, birds, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lissamphibia" title="Lissamphibia" id="mwAZU">amphibians</a>, etc.) than of other fish such as ray-finned fish or sharks, so the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Last_common_ancestor" title="Last common ancestor" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAZY">last common ancestor</a> of all fish is also an ancestor to tetrapods. As paraphyletic groups are no longer recognised in modern <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Systematics" title="Systematics" id="mwAZc">systematic biology</a>, the use of the term "fish" as a biological group must be avoided.</p>

<p id="mwAZg">Many types of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal" id="mwAZk">aquatic animals</a> commonly referred to as "fish" are not fish in the sense given above; examples include <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shellfish" title="Shellfish" id="mwAZo">shellfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish" id="mwAZs">cuttlefish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Starfish" title="Starfish" id="mwAZw">starfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crayfish" title="Crayfish" id="mwAZ0">crayfish</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish" id="mwAZ4">jellyfish</a>. In earlier times, even biologists did not make a distinction<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAZ8"> </span>– sixteenth century natural historians classified also <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pinniped" title="Pinniped" id="mwAaA">seals</a>, whales, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amphibian" title="Amphibian" id="mwAaE">amphibians</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crocodile" title="Crocodile" id="mwAaI">crocodiles</a>, even <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus" id="mwAaM">hippopotamuses</a>, as well as a host of aquatic invertebrates, as fish.<sup about="#mwt219" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-integrated_26-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"integrated"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-integrated-26"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-integrated-26" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 26;" id="mwAaQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAaU">[26]</span></a></sup> However, according to the definition above, all mammals, including <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cetacean" title="Cetacean" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAaY">cetaceans</a> like whales and dolphins, are not fish. In some contexts, especially in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture" id="mwAac">aquaculture</a>, the true fish are referred to as <b id="mwAag">finfish</b> (or <b id="mwAak">fin fish</b>) to distinguish them from these other animals.</p>

<p id="mwAao">A typical fish is <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ectothermic" title="Ectothermic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAas">ectothermic</a>, has a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Streamlines,_streaklines_and_pathlines" title="Streamlines, streaklines and pathlines" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAaw">streamlined</a> body for rapid swimming, extracts oxygen from water using gills or uses an accessory breathing organ to breathe atmospheric oxygen, has two sets of paired fins, usually one or two (rarely three) dorsal fins, an anal fin, and a tail fin, has jaws, has skin that is usually covered with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scale_(zoology)" title="Scale (zoology)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAa0">scales</a>, and lays eggs.</p>

<p id="mwAa4">Each criterion has exceptions. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tuna" title="Tuna" id="mwAa8">Tuna</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Swordfish" title="Swordfish" id="mwAbA">swordfish</a>, and some species of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shark" title="Shark" id="mwAbE">sharks</a> show <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Warm-blooded#Temperature_control_in_cold-blooded_animals" title="Warm-blooded" id="mwAbI">some warm-blooded adaptations</a><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAbM"> </span>– they can heat their bodies significantly above ambient water temperature.<sup about="#mwt225" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973_24-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"p":{"wt":"3"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey19973-24" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 24;" id="mwAbQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbU">[24]</span></a></sup> Streamlining and swimming performance varies from fish such as tuna, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Salmon" title="Salmon" id="mwAbY">salmon</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carangidae" title="Carangidae" id="mwAbc">jacks</a> that can cover 10–20 body-lengths per second to species such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Eel" title="Eel" id="mwAbg">eels</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Batoidea" title="Batoidea" id="mwAbk">rays</a> that swim no more than 0.5 body-lengths per second.<sup about="#mwt228" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997103_27-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"p":{"wt":"103"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997103-27" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 27;" id="mwAbo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbs">[27]</span></a></sup> Many groups of freshwater fish extract oxygen from the air as well as from the water using a variety of different structures. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lungfish" title="Lungfish" id="mwAbw">Lungfish</a> have paired lungs similar to those of tetrapods, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gourami" title="Gourami" id="mwAb0">gouramis</a> have a structure called the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Labyrinth_organ" title="Labyrinth organ" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAb4">labyrinth organ</a> that performs a similar function, while many catfish, such as <i id="mwAb8"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Corydoras" title="Corydoras" id="mwAcA">Corydoras</a></i> extract oxygen via the intestine or stomach.<sup about="#mwt231" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199753–57_28-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"53–57"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199753–57-28" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 28;" id="mwAcE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcI">[28]</span></a></sup> Body shape and the arrangement of the fins is highly variable, covering such seemingly un-fishlike forms as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Seahorse" title="Seahorse" id="mwAcM">seahorses</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pufferfish" title="Pufferfish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAcQ">pufferfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anglerfish" title="Anglerfish" id="mwAcU">anglerfish</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Saccopharyngiformes" title="Saccopharyngiformes" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAcY">gulpers</a>. Similarly, the surface of the skin may be naked (as in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Moray_eel" title="Moray eel" id="mwAcc">moray eels</a>), or covered with scales of a variety of different types usually defined as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placoid_scale" title="Placoid scale" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAcg">placoid</a> (typical of sharks and rays), <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scale_(zoology)#Cosmoid_scales" title="Scale (zoology)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAck">cosmoid</a> (fossil lungfish and coelacanths), <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ganoid" title="Ganoid" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAco">ganoid</a> (various fossil fish but also living <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gar" title="Gar" id="mwAcs">gars</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bichir" title="Bichir" id="mwAcw">bichirs</a>), <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_scale#Leptoid_scales" title="Fish scale" id="mwAc0">cycloid</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ctenoid" title="Ctenoid" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAc4">ctenoid</a> (these last two are found on most <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bony_fish" title="Bony fish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAc8">bony fish</a>).<sup about="#mwt234" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199733–36_29-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"33–36"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199733–36-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwAdA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdE">[29]</span></a></sup> There are even fish that live mostly on land or lay their eggs on land near water.<sup about="#mwt240" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-30" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-30"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 30;" id="mwAdI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdM">[30]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mudskipper" title="Mudskipper" id="mwAdQ">Mudskippers</a> feed and interact with one another on mudflats and go underwater to hide in their burrows.<sup about="#mwt242" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-31" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-31"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAdU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdY">[31]</span></a></sup> A single <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Undescribed_species" title="Undescribed species" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAdc">undescribed species</a> of <i id="mwAdg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phreatobius" title="Phreatobius" id="mwAdk">Phreatobius</a></i> has been called a true "land fish" as this worm-like catfish strictly lives among waterlogged <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leaf_litter" title="Leaf litter" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAdo">leaf litter</a>.<sup about="#mwt246" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-32" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-32"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 32;" id="mwAds"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdw">[32]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt250" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-33" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-33"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-33" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 33;" id="mwAd0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAd4">[33]</span></a></sup> Many species live in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Underground_lake" title="Underground lake" id="mwAd8">underground lakes</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Underground_river" title="Underground river" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAeA">underground rivers</a> or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aquifer" title="Aquifer" id="mwAeE">aquifers</a> and are popularly known as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cavefish" title="Cavefish" id="mwAeI">cavefish</a>.<sup about="#mwt254" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-34" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-34"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-34" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 34;" id="mwAeM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAeQ">[34]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAeU">Fish range in size from the huge <span about="#mwt255" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"16"},"2":{"wt":"m"},"adj":{"wt":"on"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAeY">16-metre (52</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt255"> </span><span about="#mwt255">ft)</span> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Whale_shark" title="Whale shark" id="mwAec">whale shark</a> to the tiny <span about="#mwt256" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"8"},"2":{"wt":"mm"},"sigfig":{"wt":"1"},"adj":{"wt":"on"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAeg">8-millimetre (0.3</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt256"> </span><span about="#mwt256">in)</span> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Stout_infantfish" title="Stout infantfish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAek">stout infantfish</a>.</p>

<p id="mwAeo">Fish <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Species" title="Species" id="mwAes">species</a> diversity is roughly divided equally between marine (oceanic) and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Freshwater" title="Freshwater" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAew">freshwater</a> ecosystems. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coral_reef" title="Coral reef" id="mwAe0">Coral reefs</a> in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific" id="mwAe4">Indo-Pacific</a> constitute the center of diversity for marine fishes, whereas continental freshwater fishes are most diverse in large <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./River_basin" title="River basin" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAe8">river basins</a> of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest" id="mwAfA">tropical rainforests</a>, especially the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAfE">Amazon</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin" id="mwAfI">Congo</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mekong" title="Mekong" id="mwAfM">Mekong</a> basins. More than 5,600 fish species inhabit <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neotropic" title="Neotropic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAfQ">Neotropical</a> freshwaters alone, such that <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neotropical_fish" title="Neotropical fish" id="mwAfU">Neotropical fishes</a> represent about 10% of all <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate" id="mwAfY">vertebrate</a> species on the Earth. Exceptionally rich sites in the Amazon basin, such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cantão_State_Park" title="Cantão State Park" id="mwAfc">Cantão State Park</a>, can contain more freshwater fish species than occur in all of Europe.<sup about="#mwt262" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-35" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-35"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-35" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 35;" id="mwAfg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAfk">[35]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAfo">The deepest living fish in the ocean so far found is the Mariana snailfish (<i id="mwAfs"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pseudoliparis_swirei" title="Pseudoliparis swirei" id="mwAfw">Pseudoliparis swirei</a></i>) which lives at deeps of 8,000 meters (26,200 feet) along the Mariana Trench near Guam.<sup about="#mwt267" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-36" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-36"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-36" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mwAf0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAf4">[36]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAf8">The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity" id="mwAgA">diversity</a> of living fish (finfish) is unevenly distributed among the various groups, with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Teleosts" title="Teleosts" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAgE">teleosts</a> making up the bulk of living fishes (96%), and over 50% of all <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate" id="mwAgI">vertebrate</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Species" title="Species" id="mwAgM">species</a>.<sup about="#mwt271" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto_21-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-auto-21" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 21;" id="mwAgQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgU">[21]</span></a></sup> The following <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cladogram" title="Cladogram" id="mwAgY">cladogram</a><sup about="#mwt275" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Friedman_&amp;_Sallan_2012_37-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Friedman &amp; Sallan 2012"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Friedman_&amp;_Sallan_2012-37"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Friedman_&amp;_Sallan_2012-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 37;" id="mwAgc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgg">[37]</span></a></sup> shows the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAgk">evolutionary relationships</a> of all groups of living fishes (with their respective diversity<sup about="#mwt276" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto_21-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-auto-21" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 21;" id="mwAgo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgs">[21]</span></a></sup>) and the four-limbed vertebrates (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapods" title="Tetrapods" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAgw">tetrapods</a>).</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAg0"><a href="./File:Fish_evolution.png" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAg4"><img resource="./File:Fish_evolution.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Fish_evolution.png/220px-Fish_evolution.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="1073" data-file-height="767" data-file-type="bitmap" height="157" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Fish_evolution.png/330px-Fish_evolution.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Fish_evolution.png/440px-Fish_evolution.png 2x" id="mwAg8"/></a><figcaption id="mwAhA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity" id="mwAhE">Diversity</a> of various groups of fish (and other <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAhI">vertebrates</a>) through time</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAhM"><a href="./File:Queensland_Lungfish_(Neoceratodus_forsteri).jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAhQ"><img resource="./File:Queensland_Lungfish_(Neoceratodus_forsteri).jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg/220px-Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="400" data-file-type="bitmap" height="125" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg/330px-Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg/440px-Queensland_Lungfish_%28Neoceratodus_forsteri%29.jpg 2x" id="mwAhU"/></a><figcaption id="mwAhY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lungfish" title="Lungfish" id="mwAhc">Lungfish</a> are the closest living relatives of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapods" title="Tetrapods" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAhg">tetrapods</a> (four-limbed vertebrates).</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAhk"><a href="./File:Amia_calva_4.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAho"><img resource="./File:Amia_calva_4.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amia_calva_4.jpg/220px-Amia_calva_4.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="475" data-file-type="bitmap" height="87" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amia_calva_4.jpg/330px-Amia_calva_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Amia_calva_4.jpg/440px-Amia_calva_4.jpg 2x" id="mwAhs"/></a><figcaption id="mwAhw">The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bowfin" title="Bowfin" id="mwAh0">bowfin</a> <i id="mwAh4">Amia calva</i> is the sole survivor of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Halecomorph" title="Halecomorph" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAh8">halecomorph</a> clade.</figcaption></figure>

<section data-mw-section-id="7" id="mwAjQ"><h3 id="Respiration">Respiration</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt316" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"See also","href":"./Template:See_also"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Aquatic respiration"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAjU"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt316" id="mwAjY">See also: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aquatic_respiration" title="Aquatic respiration">Aquatic respiration</a></div>

<section data-mw-section-id="8" id="mwAjc"><h4 id="Gills">Gills</h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAjg"><a href="./File:Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAjk"><img alt="Photo of fish head split in half longitudinally with gill filaments crossing from top to bottom" resource="./File:Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg/220px-Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1103" data-file-height="954" data-file-type="bitmap" height="190" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg/330px-Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg/440px-Tuna_Gills_in_Situ_01.jpg 2x" id="mwAjo"/></a><figcaption id="mwAjs"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tuna" title="Tuna" id="mwAjw">Tuna</a> gills inside the head. The fish head is oriented snout-downwards, with the view looking towards the mouth.</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwAj0">Most fish exchange gases using <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gill" title="Gill" id="mwAj4">gills</a> on either side of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pharynx" title="Pharynx" id="mwAj8">pharynx</a>. Gills consist of threadlike structures called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Protein_filament" title="Protein filament" id="mwAkA">filaments</a>. Each filament contains a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Capillary" title="Capillary" id="mwAkE">capillary</a> network that provides a large <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Surface_area" title="Surface area" id="mwAkI">surface area</a> for exchanging <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oxygen" title="Oxygen" id="mwAkM">oxygen</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide" id="mwAkQ">carbon dioxide</a>. Fish exchange gases by pulling oxygen-rich water through their mouths and pumping it over their gills. In some fish, capillary blood flows in the opposite direction to the water, causing <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Countercurrent_exchange" title="Countercurrent exchange" id="mwAkU">countercurrent exchange</a>. The gills push the oxygen-poor water out through openings in the sides of the pharynx. Some fish, like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shark" title="Shark" id="mwAkY">sharks</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwAkc">lampreys</a>, possess multiple gill openings. However, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bony_fish" title="Bony fish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAkg">bony fish</a> have a single gill opening on each side. This opening is hidden beneath a protective bony cover called an <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Operculum_(fish)" title="Operculum (fish)" id="mwAkk">operculum</a>.</p>

<p id="mwAko">Juvenile <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bichir" title="Bichir" id="mwAks">bichirs</a> have external gills, a very primitive feature that they share with larval <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amphibian" title="Amphibian" id="mwAkw">amphibians</a>.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="9" id="mwAk0"><h4 id="Air_breathing">Air breathing</h4>
<p id="mwAk4">Fish from multiple groups can live out of the water for extended periods. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amphibious_fish" title="Amphibious fish" id="mwAk8">Amphibious fish</a> such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mudskipper" title="Mudskipper" id="mwAlA">mudskipper</a> can live and move about on land for up to several days,<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_accuracy_disputes" about="#mwt320" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"doubtful","href":"./Template:Doubtful"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"December 2014"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAlE"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_disputed_statements_from_December_2014" about="#mwt320"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template " style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt320" id="mwAlI"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (December 2014)">dubious</span></a><span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span><span class="metadata"> <span typeof="mw:Entity">–</span> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Talk:Fish#Dubious" title="Talk:Fish">discuss</a></span></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> or live in stagnant or otherwise oxygen-depleted water. Many such fish can breathe air via a variety of mechanisms. The skin of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anguillidae" title="Anguillidae" id="mwAlM">anguillid eels</a> may absorb oxygen directly. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Buccal_cavity" title="Buccal cavity" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAlQ">buccal cavity</a> of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Electric_eel" title="Electric eel" id="mwAlU">electric eel</a> may breathe air. Catfish of the families <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Loricariidae" title="Loricariidae" id="mwAlY">Loricariidae</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Callichthyidae" title="Callichthyidae" id="mwAlc">Callichthyidae</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scoloplacidae" title="Scoloplacidae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAlg">Scoloplacidae</a> absorb air through their digestive tracts.<sup about="#mwt324" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Armbruster1998_38-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Armbruster1998"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Armbruster1998-38"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Armbruster1998-38" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAlk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAlo">[38]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lungfish" title="Lungfish" id="mwAls">Lungfish</a>, with the exception of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Australian_lungfish" title="Australian lungfish" id="mwAlw">Australian lungfish</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bichir" title="Bichir" id="mwAl0">bichirs</a> have paired lungs similar to those of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod" id="mwAl4">tetrapods</a> and must surface to gulp fresh air through the mouth and pass spent air out through the gills. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gar" title="Gar" id="mwAl8">Gar</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bowfin" title="Bowfin" id="mwAmA">bowfin</a> have a vascularized swim bladder that functions in the same way. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cypriniformes" title="Cypriniformes" id="mwAmE">Loaches</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Erythrinidae" title="Erythrinidae" id="mwAmI">trahiras</a>, and many <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Catfish" title="Catfish" id="mwAmM">catfish</a> breathe by passing air through the gut. Mudskippers breathe by absorbing oxygen across the skin (similar to frogs). A number of fish have evolved so-called accessory breathing organs that extract oxygen from the air. Labyrinth fish (such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gourami" title="Gourami" id="mwAmQ">gouramis</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Betta" title="Betta" id="mwAmU">bettas</a>) have a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Labyrinth_(organ)" title="Labyrinth (organ)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAmY">labyrinth organ</a> above the gills that performs this function. A few other fish have structures resembling labyrinth organs in form and function, most notably <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Snakehead_(fish)" title="Snakehead (fish)" id="mwAmc">snakeheads</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Luciocephalidae" title="Luciocephalidae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAmg">pikeheads</a>, and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Clariidae" title="Clariidae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAmk">Clariidae</a> catfish family.</p>

<p id="mwAmo">Breathing air is primarily of use to fish that inhabit shallow, seasonally variable waters where the water's oxygen concentration may seasonally decline. Fish dependent solely on dissolved oxygen, such as perch and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cichlid" title="Cichlid" id="mwAms">cichlids</a>, quickly suffocate, while air-breathers survive for much longer, in some cases in water that is little more than wet mud. At the extreme, some air-breathing fish are able to survive in damp burrows for weeks without water, entering a state of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aestivation" title="Aestivation" id="mwAmw">aestivation</a> (summertime hibernation) until water returns.</p>

<p id="mwAm0">Air breathing fish can be divided into obligate air breathers and facultative air breathers. Obligate air breathers, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./African_lungfish" title="African lungfish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAm4">African lungfish</a>, <i id="mwAm8">must</i> breathe air periodically or they suffocate. Facultative air breathers, such as the catfish <i id="mwAnA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hypostomus_plecostomus" title="Hypostomus plecostomus" id="mwAnE">Hypostomus plecostomus</a></i>, only breathe air if they need to and will otherwise rely on their gills for oxygen. Most air breathing fish are facultative air breathers that avoid the energetic cost of rising to the surface and the fitness cost of exposure to surface predators.<sup about="#mwt326" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Armbruster1998_38-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Armbruster1998"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Armbruster1998-38" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAnI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAnM">[38]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="10" id="mwAnQ"><h3 id="Circulation">Circulation</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAnU"><a href="./File:Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAnY"><img resource="./File:Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg/170px-Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg" decoding="async" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3872" data-file-type="bitmap" height="254" width="170" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg/255px-Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg/340px-Didactic_model_of_a_fish_heart--FMVZ_USP-09.jpeg 2x" id="mwAnc"/></a><figcaption id="mwAng"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Educational_toy" title="Educational toy" id="mwAnk">Didactic model</a> of a fish heart</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwAno">Fish have a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system" id="mwAns">closed-loop circulatory system</a>. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Heart" title="Heart" id="mwAnw">heart</a> pumps the blood in a single loop throughout the body. In most fish, the heart consists of four parts, including two chambers and an entrance and exit.<sup about="#mwt331" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Setaro_39-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Setaro"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Setaro-39"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Setaro-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 39;" id="mwAn0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAn4">[39]</span></a></sup> The first part is the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sinus_venosus" title="Sinus venosus" id="mwAn8">sinus venosus</a>, a thin-walled sac that collects blood from the fish's <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vein" title="Vein" id="mwAoA">veins</a> before allowing it to flow to the second part, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)" id="mwAoE">atrium</a>, which is a large muscular chamber. The atrium serves as a one-way antechamber, sends blood to the third part, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)" id="mwAoI">ventricle</a>. The ventricle is another thick-walled, muscular chamber and it pumps the blood, first to the fourth part, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bulbus_arteriosus" title="Bulbus arteriosus" id="mwAoM">bulbus arteriosus</a>, a large tube, and then out of the heart. The bulbus arteriosus connects to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aorta" title="Aorta" id="mwAoQ">aorta</a>, through which blood flows to the gills for oxygenation.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="11" id="mwAoU"><h3 id="Digestion">Digestion</h3>
<p id="mwAoY">Jaws allow fish to eat a wide variety of food, including plants and other organisms. Fish ingest food through the mouth and break it down in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Esophagus" title="Esophagus" id="mwAoc">esophagus</a>. In the stomach, food is further digested and, in many fish, processed in finger-shaped pouches called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pyloric_caeca" title="Pyloric caeca" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAog">pyloric caeca</a>, which secrete digestive <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Enzyme" title="Enzyme" id="mwAok">enzymes</a> and absorb nutrients. Organs such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Liver" title="Liver" id="mwAoo">liver</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pancreas" title="Pancreas" id="mwAos">pancreas</a> add enzymes and various chemicals as the food moves through the digestive tract. The intestine completes the process of digestion and nutrient absorption.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="12" id="mwAow"><h3 id="Excretion">Excretion</h3>
<p id="mwAo0">As with many aquatic animals, most fish release their nitrogenous wastes as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ammonia" title="Ammonia" id="mwAo4">ammonia</a>. Some of the wastes <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diffusion" title="Diffusion" id="mwAo8">diffuse</a> through the gills. Blood wastes are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Filter_(chemistry)" title="Filter (chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwApA">filtered</a> by the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kidney" title="Kidney" id="mwApE">kidneys</a>.</p>

<p id="mwApI">Saltwater fish tend to lose water because of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osmosis" title="Osmosis" id="mwApM">osmosis</a>. Their kidneys return water to the body. The reverse happens in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Freshwater_fish" title="Freshwater fish" id="mwApQ">freshwater fish</a>: they tend to gain water osmotically. Their kidneys produce dilute urine for excretion. Some fish have specially adapted kidneys that vary in function, allowing them to move from freshwater to saltwater.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="13" id="mwApU"><h3 id="Scales">Scales</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt332" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fish scale"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwApY"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt332" id="mwApc">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_scale" title="Fish scale">Fish scale</a></div>
<p id="mwApg">The scales of fish originate from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm" id="mwApk">mesoderm</a> (skin); they may be similar in structure to teeth.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="14" id="mwApo"><h3 id="Sensory_and_nervous_system">Sensory and nervous system</h3>
