<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAps"><a href="./File:Fish_brain.png" class="mw-file-description" id="mwApw"><img alt="Anatomical diagram showing the pairs of olfactory, telencephalon, and optic lobes, followed by the cerebellum and the mylencephalon" resource="./File:Fish_brain.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fish_brain.png/170px-Fish_brain.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="690" data-file-height="1206" data-file-type="bitmap" height="297" width="170" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fish_brain.png/255px-Fish_brain.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Fish_brain.png/340px-Fish_brain.png 2x" id="mwAp0"/></a><figcaption id="mwAp4">Dorsal view of the brain of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout" id="mwAp8">rainbow trout</a></figcaption></figure>

<section data-mw-section-id="15" id="mwAqA"><h4 id="Central_nervous_system">Central nervous system</h4>
<p id="mwAqE">Fish typically have quite small brains relative to body size compared with other vertebrates, typically one-fifteenth the brain mass of a similarly sized bird or mammal.<sup about="#mwt335" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAqI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqM">[40]</span></a></sup> However, some fish have relatively large brains, most notably <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mormyridae" title="Mormyridae" id="mwAqQ">mormyrids</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shark" title="Shark" id="mwAqU">sharks</a>, which have brains about as massive relative to body weight as birds and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Marsupial" title="Marsupial" id="mwAqY">marsupials</a>.<sup about="#mwt338" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997191_41-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"p":{"wt":"191"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997191-41" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 41;" id="mwAqc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqg">[41]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAqk">Fish brains are divided into several regions. At the front are the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb" id="mwAqo">olfactory lobes</a>, a pair of structures that receive and process signals from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nostril" title="Nostril" id="mwAqs">nostrils</a> via the two <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Olfactory_nerve" title="Olfactory nerve" id="mwAqw">olfactory nerves</a>.<sup about="#mwt341" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAq0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAq4">[40]</span></a></sup> The olfactory lobes are very large in fish that hunt primarily by smell, such as hagfish, sharks, and catfish. Behind the olfactory lobes is the two-lobed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Telencephalon" title="Telencephalon" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAq8">telencephalon</a>, the structural equivalent to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum" id="mwArA">cerebrum</a> in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amniote" title="Amniote" id="mwArE">higher vertebrates</a>. In fish the telencephalon is concerned mostly with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Olfaction" title="Olfaction" class="mw-redirect" id="mwArI">olfaction</a>.<sup about="#mwt344" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwArM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwArQ">[40]</span></a></sup> Together these structures form the forebrain.</p>

<p id="mwArU">Connecting the forebrain to the midbrain is the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diencephalon" title="Diencephalon" id="mwArY">diencephalon</a> (in the diagram, this structure is below the optic lobes and consequently not visible). The diencephalon performs functions associated with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hormone" title="Hormone" id="mwArc">hormones</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis" id="mwArg">homeostasis</a>.<sup about="#mwt347" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwArk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAro">[40]</span></a></sup> The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pineal_body" title="Pineal body" class="mw-redirect" id="mwArs">pineal body</a> lies just above the diencephalon. This structure detects light, maintains <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Circadian" title="Circadian" class="mw-redirect" id="mwArw">circadian</a> rhythms, and controls color changes.<sup about="#mwt350" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAr0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAr4">[40]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAr8">The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Midbrain" title="Midbrain" id="mwAsA">midbrain</a> (or mesencephalon) contains the two <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Midbrain#Corpora_quadrigemina" title="Midbrain" id="mwAsE">optic lobes</a>. These are very large in species that hunt by sight, such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout" id="mwAsI">rainbow trout</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cichlid" title="Cichlid" id="mwAsM">cichlids</a>.<sup about="#mwt353" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAsQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAsU">[40]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAsY">The hindbrain (or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Metencephalon" title="Metencephalon" id="mwAsc">metencephalon</a>) is particularly involved in swimming and balance.<sup about="#mwt356" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-6" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAsg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAsk">[40]</span></a></sup> The cerebellum is a single-lobed structure that is typically the biggest part of the brain.<sup about="#mwt359" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-7" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAso"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAss">[40]</span></a></sup> Hagfish and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwAsw">lampreys</a> have relatively small cerebellae, while the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mormyrid" title="Mormyrid" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAs0">mormyrid</a> cerebellum is massive and apparently involved in their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Electric_fish" title="Electric fish" id="mwAs4">electrical sense</a>.<sup about="#mwt362" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-8" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAs8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtA">[40]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAtE">The brain stem (or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Myelencephalon" title="Myelencephalon" id="mwAtI">myelencephalon</a>) is the brain's posterior.<sup about="#mwt365" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-9" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAtM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtQ">[40]</span></a></sup> As well as controlling some muscles and body organs, in bony fish at least, the brain stem governs <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)" id="mwAtU">respiration</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osmoregulation" title="Osmoregulation" id="mwAtY">osmoregulation</a>.<sup about="#mwt368" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49_40-10" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"48–49"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199748–49-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAtc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtg">[40]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="16" id="mwAtk"><h4 id="Sense_organs">Sense organs</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt371" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Sensory systems in fish"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAto"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt371" id="mwAts">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sensory_systems_in_fish" title="Sensory systems in fish">Sensory systems in fish</a></div>

<p id="mwAtw">Most fish possess highly developed sense organs. Nearly all daylight fish have color vision that is at least as good as a human's (see <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vision_in_fishes" title="Vision in fishes" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAt0">vision in fishes</a>). Many fish also have chemoreceptors that are responsible for extraordinary senses of taste and smell. Although they have ears, many fish may not hear very well. Most fish have sensitive receptors that form the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lateral_line_system" title="Lateral line system" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAt4">lateral line system</a>, which detects gentle currents and vibrations, and senses the motion of nearby fish and prey.<sup about="#mwt380" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Encarta_99_42-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Encarta 99"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Encarta_99-42"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Encarta_99-42" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 42;" id="mwAt8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAuA">[42]</span></a></sup> The sense information obtained from the lateral line system can be considered both a sense of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Touch" title="Touch" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAuE">touch</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hearing" title="Hearing" id="mwAuI">hearing</a>. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Blind_cave_fish" title="Blind cave fish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAuM">Blind cave fish</a> navigate almost entirely through the sensations from their lateral line system.<sup about="#mwt384" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-43" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-43"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-43" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 43;" id="mwAuQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAuU">[43]</span></a></sup>  Some fish, such as catfish and sharks, have the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ampullae_of_Lorenzini" title="Ampullae of Lorenzini" id="mwAuY">ampullae of Lorenzini</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Electroreceptor" title="Electroreceptor" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAuc">electroreceptors</a> that detect weak electric currents on the order of millivolt.<sup about="#mwt385" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-44" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-44"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-44" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 44;" id="mwAug"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAuk">[44]</span></a></sup> Other fish, like the South American electric fishes <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gymnotiformes" title="Gymnotiformes" id="mwAuo">Gymnotiformes</a>, can produce weak electric currents, which they use in navigation and social communication.</p>

<p id="mwAus">Fish orient themselves using landmarks and may use mental maps based on multiple landmarks or symbols. Fish behavior in mazes reveals that they possess spatial memory and visual discrimination.<sup about="#mwt390" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-45" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-45"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-45" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 45;" id="mwAuw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAu0">[45]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="17" id="mwAu4"><h4 id="Vision">Vision</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt391" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Vision in fishes"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAu8"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt391" id="mwAvA">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vision_in_fishes" title="Vision in fishes" class="mw-redirect">Vision in fishes</a></div>
<p id="mwAvE"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Visual_system" title="Visual system" id="mwAvI">Vision</a> is an important <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sensory_system" title="Sensory system" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAvM">sensory system</a> for most species of fish. Fish eyes are similar to those of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal" id="mwAvQ">terrestrial</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate" id="mwAvU">vertebrates</a> like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bird_vision" title="Bird vision" id="mwAvY">birds</a> and mammals, but have a more <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Spherical" title="Spherical" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAvc">spherical</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAvg">lens</a>. Their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Retina" title="Retina" id="mwAvk">retinas</a> generally have both <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Rod_cell" title="Rod cell" id="mwAvo">rods</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cone_cell" title="Cone cell" id="mwAvs">cones</a> (for <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scotopic_vision" title="Scotopic vision" id="mwAvw">scotopic</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Photopic_vision" title="Photopic vision" id="mwAv0">photopic vision</a>), and most species have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Colour_vision" title="Colour vision" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAv4">colour vision</a>. Some fish can see <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet" id="mwAv8">ultraviolet</a> and some can see <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Polarized_light" title="Polarized light" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAwA">polarized light</a>. Amongst <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jawless_fish" title="Jawless fish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAwE">jawless fish</a>, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwAwI">lamprey</a> has well-developed eyes, while the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hagfish" title="Hagfish" id="mwAwM">hagfish</a> has only primitive <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Eyespot_(mimicry)" title="Eyespot (mimicry)" id="mwAwQ">eyespots</a>.<sup about="#mwt398" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-46" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-46"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-46" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 46;" id="mwAwU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAwY">[46]</span></a></sup> Fish vision shows <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Adaptation" title="Adaptation" id="mwAwc">adaptation</a> to their visual environment, for example <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Deep_sea_fish" title="Deep sea fish" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAwg">deep sea fishes</a> have eyes suited to the dark environment.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="18" id="mwAwk"><h4 id="Hearing">Hearing</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt399" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"see also","href":"./Template:See_also"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Hearing in fish"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAwo"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt399" id="mwAws">See also: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hearing_in_fish" title="Hearing in fish" class="mw-redirect">Hearing in fish</a></div>

<p id="mwAww"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hearing_(sense)" title="Hearing (sense)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAw0">Hearing</a> is an important sensory system for most species of fish. Fish sense sound using their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lateral_line" title="Lateral line" id="mwAw4">lateral lines</a> and their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Otolith" title="Otolith" id="mwAw8">ears</a>.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="19" id="mwAxA"><h4 id="Cognition">Cognition</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt402" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fish intelligence"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAxE"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt402" id="mwAxI">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_intelligence" title="Fish intelligence">Fish intelligence</a></div>

<p id="mwAxM">New research has expanded preconceptions about the cognitive capacities of fish. For example, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Manta_rays" title="Manta rays" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAxQ">manta rays</a> have exhibited behavior linked to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Self-awareness" title="Self-awareness" id="mwAxU">self-awareness</a> in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mirror_test" title="Mirror test" id="mwAxY">mirror test</a> cases. Placed in front of a mirror, individual rays engaged in contingency testing, that is, repetitive behavior aiming to check whether their reflection's behavior mimics their body movement.<sup about="#mwt409" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-47" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-47"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-47" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 47;" id="mwAxc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAxg">[47]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAxk"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wrasses" title="Wrasses" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAxo">Wrasses</a> have also passed the mirror test in a 2018 scientific study.<sup about="#mwt416" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-48" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-48"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-48" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 48;" id="mwAxs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAxw">[48]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt420" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-49" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-49"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-49" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 49;" id="mwAx0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAx4">[49]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAx8">Cases of tool use have also been noticed, notably in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Choerodon" title="Choerodon" id="mwAyA">Choerodon</a> family, in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Archerfish" title="Archerfish" id="mwAyE">archerfish</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod" id="mwAyI">Atlantic cod</a>.<sup about="#mwt423" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-50" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-50"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-50" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 50;" id="mwAyM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAyQ">[50]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="20" id="mwAyU"><h4 id="Capacity_for_pain">Capacity for pain</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt424" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Pain in fish"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAyY"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt424" id="mwAyc">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pain_in_fish" title="Pain in fish">Pain in fish</a></div>

<p id="mwAyg">Experiments done by William Tavolga provide evidence that fish have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pain" title="Pain" id="mwAyk">pain</a> and fear responses. For instance, in Tavolga's experiments, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Batrachoididae" title="Batrachoididae" id="mwAyo">toadfish</a> grunted when electrically shocked and over time they came to grunt at the mere sight of an electrode.<sup about="#mwt428" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-51" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-51"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-51" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 51;" id="mwAys"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAyw">[51]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAy0">In 2003, Scottish scientists at the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh" id="mwAy4">University of Edinburgh</a> and the Roslin Institute concluded that rainbow trout exhibit behaviors often associated with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pain" title="Pain" id="mwAy8">pain</a> in other animals. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bee" title="Bee" id="mwAzA">Bee</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Venom" title="Venom" id="mwAzE">venom</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid" id="mwAzI">acetic acid</a> injected into the lips resulted in fish rocking their bodies and rubbing their lips along the sides and floors of their tanks, which the researchers concluded were attempts to relieve pain, similar to what mammals would do.<sup about="#mwt435" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-52" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-52"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-52" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 52;" id="mwAzM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzQ">[52]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt439" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-grandin183_53-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"grandin183"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-grandin183-53"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-grandin183-53" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwAzU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzY">[53]</span></a></sup> Neurons fired in a pattern resembling human neuronal patterns.<sup about="#mwt440" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-grandin183_53-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"grandin183"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-grandin183-53" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwAzc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzg">[53]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAzk">Professor James D. Rose of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./University_of_Wyoming" title="University of Wyoming" id="mwAzo">University of Wyoming</a> claimed the study was flawed since it did not provide proof that fish possess "conscious awareness, particularly a kind of awareness that is meaningfully like ours".<sup about="#mwt447" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-54" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-54"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-54" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 54;" id="mwAzs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzw">[54]</span></a></sup> Rose argues that since fish brains are so different from human brains, fish are probably not conscious in the manner humans are, so that reactions similar to human reactions to pain instead have other causes. Rose had published a study a year earlier arguing that fish cannot feel pain because their brains lack a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neocortex" title="Neocortex" id="mwAz0">neocortex</a>.<sup about="#mwt451" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-55" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-55"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-55" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 55;" id="mwAz4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAz8">[55]</span></a></sup> However, animal behaviorist <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Temple_Grandin" title="Temple Grandin" id="mwA0A">Temple Grandin</a> argues that fish could still have consciousness without a neocortex because "different species can use different brain structures and systems to handle the same functions."<sup about="#mwt452" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-grandin183_53-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"grandin183"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-grandin183-53" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwA0E"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA0I">[53]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA0M">Animal welfare advocates raise concerns about the possible <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Suffering" title="Suffering" id="mwA0Q">suffering</a> of fish caused by angling. Some countries, such as Germany, have banned specific types of fishing, and the British <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./RSPCA" title="RSPCA" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA0U">RSPCA</a> now formally prosecutes individuals who are cruel to fish.<sup about="#mwt457" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-56" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-56"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-56" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 56;" id="mwA0Y"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA0c">[56]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="21" id="mwA0g"><h4 id="Emotion">Emotion</h4>
<p id="mwA0k">In 2019, scientists have shown that members of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Monogamous" title="Monogamous" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA0o">monogamous</a> species <i id="mwA0s"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amatitlania_siquia" title="Amatitlania siquia" id="mwA0w">Amatitlania siquia</a></i> exhibit pessimistic behavior when they are prevented from being with their partner.<sup about="#mwt462" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-57" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-57"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-57" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 57;" id="mwA00"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA04">[57]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="22" id="mwA08"><h3 id="Muscular_system">Muscular system</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt463" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fish locomotion"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwA1A"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt463" id="mwA1E">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_locomotion" title="Fish locomotion">Fish locomotion</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwA1I"><a href="./File:Lampanyctodes_hectoris_(Hector's_lanternfish).svg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwA1M"><img resource="./File:Lampanyctodes_hectoris_(Hector's_lanternfish).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg/330px-Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="747" data-file-height="288" data-file-type="drawing" height="127" width="330" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg/495px-Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg/660px-Lampanyctodes_hectoris_%28Hector%27s_lanternfish%29.svg.png 2x" id="mwA1Q"/></a><figcaption id="mwA1U">The anatomy of <i id="mwA1Y">Lampanyctodes hectoris</i> (1)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1c"> </span>operculum (gill cover), (2)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1g"> </span>lateral line, (3)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1k"> </span>dorsal fin, (4)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1o"> </span>fat fin, (5)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1s"> </span>caudal peduncle, (6)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1w"> </span>caudal fin, (7)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA10"> </span>anal fin, (8)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA14"> </span>photophores, (9)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA18"> </span>pelvic fins (paired), (10)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA2A"> </span>pectoral fins (paired)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwA2E"><a href="./File:Swim_bladder.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwA2I"><img alt="Photo of white bladder that consists of a rectangular section and a banana-shaped section connected by a much thinner element" resource="./File:Swim_bladder.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Swim_bladder.jpg/220px-Swim_bladder.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="235" data-file-type="bitmap" height="65" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Swim_bladder.jpg/330px-Swim_bladder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Swim_bladder.jpg/440px-Swim_bladder.jpg 2x" id="mwA2M"/></a><figcaption id="mwA2Q">Swim bladder of a rudd (<i id="mwA2U"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scardinius_erythrophthalmus" title="Scardinius erythrophthalmus" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA2Y">Scardinius erythrophthalmus</a></i>)</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwA2c">Most fish move by alternately contracting paired sets of muscles on either side of the backbone. These contractions form S-shaped curves that move down the body. As each curve reaches the back fin, backward force is applied to the water, and in conjunction with the fins, moves the fish forward. The fish's fins function like an airplane's flaps. Fins also increase the tail's surface area, increasing speed. The streamlined body of the fish decreases the amount of friction from the water. Since body tissue is denser than water, fish must compensate for the difference or they will sink. Many bony fish have an internal organ called a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Swim_bladder" title="Swim bladder" id="mwA2g">swim bladder</a> that adjusts their buoyancy through manipulation of gases.</p>

<section data-mw-section-id="23" id="mwA2k"><h4 id="Endothermy">Endothermy</h4>
<p id="mwA2o">Although most fish are exclusively <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ectothermic" title="Ectothermic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA2s">ectothermic</a>, there are exceptions. The only known bony fishes (infraclass <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Teleostei" title="Teleostei" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA2w">Teleostei</a>) that exhibit <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded" id="mwA20">endothermy</a> are in the suborder <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scombroidei" title="Scombroidei" id="mwA24">Scombroidei</a> – which includes the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Billfish" title="Billfish" id="mwA28">billfishes</a>, tunas, and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Butterfly_kingfish" title="Butterfly kingfish" id="mwA3A">butterfly kingfish</a>, a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)" id="mwA3E">basal</a> species of mackerel<sup about="#mwt474" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Block1993_58-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Block1993"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Block1993-58"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Block1993-58" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 58;" id="mwA3I"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA3M">[58]</span></a></sup> – and also the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lampris_guttatus" title="Lampris guttatus" id="mwA3Q">opah</a>. The opah, a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lampriform" title="Lampriform" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA3U">lampriform</a>, was demonstrated in 2015 to use "whole-body endothermy", generating heat with its swimming muscles to warm its body while countercurrent exchange (as in respiration) minimizes heat loss.<sup about="#mwt478" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-59" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-59"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-59" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 59;" id="mwA3Y"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA3c">[59]</span></a></sup> It is able to actively hunt prey such as squid and swim for long distances due to the ability to warm its entire body, including its heart,<sup about="#mwt482" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-60" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-60"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-60" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 60;" id="mwA3g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA3k">[60]</span></a></sup> which is a trait typically found in only mammals and birds (in the form of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Homeothermy" title="Homeothermy" id="mwA3o">homeothermy</a>).<!-- Mentioned in Wegner 2015 --> In the cartilaginous fishes (class <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chondrichthyes" title="Chondrichthyes" id="mwA3s">Chondrichthyes</a>), sharks of the families <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamnidae" title="Lamnidae" id="mwA3w">Lamnidae</a> (porbeagle, mackerel, salmon, and great white sharks) and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Alopiidae" title="Alopiidae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA30">Alopiidae</a> (thresher sharks) exhibit endothermy. The degree of endothermy varies from the billfishes, which warm only their eyes and brain, to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna" id="mwA34">bluefin tuna</a> and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Porbeagle_shark" title="Porbeagle shark" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA38">porbeagle shark</a>, which maintain body temperatures in excess of <span about="#mwt470" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"20"},"2":{"wt":"C"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwA4A">20</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt470"> </span><span about="#mwt470">°C (68</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt470"> </span><span about="#mwt470">°F)</span> above ambient water temperatures.<sup about="#mwt483" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Block1993_58-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Block1993"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Block1993-58" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 58;" id="mwA4E"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA4I">[58]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA4M">Endothermy, though metabolically costly, is thought to provide advantages such as increased muscle strength, higher rates of central <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nervous_system" title="Nervous system" id="mwA4Q">nervous system</a> processing, and higher rates of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Digestion" title="Digestion" id="mwA4U">digestion</a>.</p>

<p id="mwA4Y"><span class="anchor" id="Reproductive_method" about="#mwt484" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Anchor","href":"./Template:Anchor"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Reproductive method"}},"i":0}}]}'></span></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="24" id="mwA4c"><h3 id="Reproductive_system">Reproductive system</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt485" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fish reproduction"},"2":{"wt":"Spawn (biology)"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwA4g"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt485" id="mwA4k">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_reproduction" title="Fish reproduction">Fish reproduction</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Spawn_(biology)" title="Spawn (biology)">Spawn (biology)</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwA4o"><a href="./File:Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwA4s"><img resource="./File:Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_(cropped).jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="2240" data-file-height="2800" data-file-type="bitmap" height="213" width="170" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Fish_ovary-_FMVZ_USP-27_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" id="mwA4w"/></a><figcaption id="mwA40">Ovary of fish (Corumbatá)</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwA44">Fish reproductive organs include <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Testicle" title="Testicle" id="mwA48">testicles</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovary" title="Ovary" id="mwA5A">ovaries</a>. In most species, gonads are paired organs of similar size, which can be partially or totally fused.<sup about="#mwt489" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Guimaraes"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Guimaraes-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 61;" id="mwA5E"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA5I">[61]</span></a></sup> There may also be a range of secondary organs that increase reproductive fitness.</p>

<p id="mwA5M">In terms of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Spermatogonia" title="Spermatogonia" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA5Q">spermatogonia</a> distribution, the structure of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Teleost" title="Teleost" id="mwA5U">teleosts</a> testes has two types: in the most common, spermatogonia occur all along the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Seminiferous_tubule" title="Seminiferous tubule" id="mwA5Y">seminiferous tubules</a>, while in <a rel="mw:WikiLink/Interwiki" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/atherinomorph" title="wikt:atherinomorph" class="extiw" id="mwA5c">atherinomorph</a> fish they are confined to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anatomical_terms_of_location#Proximal_and_distal" title="Anatomical terms of location" id="mwA5g">distal</a> portion of these structures. Fish can present cystic or semi-cystic <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Spermatogenesis" title="Spermatogenesis" id="mwA5k">spermatogenesis</a> in relation to the release phase of germ cells in cysts to the seminiferous tubules <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lumen_(anatomy)" title="Lumen (anatomy)" id="mwA5o">lumen</a>.<sup about="#mwt494" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Guimaraes"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Guimaraes-61"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Guimaraes-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 61;" id="mwA5s"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA5w">[61]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA50">Fish ovaries may be of three types: gymnovarian, secondary gymnovarian or cystovarian. In the first type, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oocyte" title="Oocyte" id="mwA54">oocytes</a> are released directly into the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coelom" title="Coelom" id="mwA58">coelomic</a> cavity and then enter the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ostium_of_Fallopian_tube" title="Ostium of Fallopian tube" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA6A">ostium</a>, then through the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oviduct" title="Oviduct" id="mwA6E">oviduct</a> and are eliminated. Secondary gymnovarian ovaries shed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovum" title="Ovum" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA6I">ova</a> into the coelom from which they go directly into the oviduct. In the third type, the oocytes are conveyed to the exterior through the oviduct.<sup about="#mwt500" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Brito_62-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Brito"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Brito-62"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Brito-62" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 62;" id="mwA6M"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA6Q">[62]</span></a></sup> Gymnovaries are the primitive condition found in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lungfish" title="Lungfish" id="mwA6U">lungfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon" id="mwA6Y">sturgeon</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bowfin" title="Bowfin" id="mwA6c">bowfin</a>. Cystovaries characterize most teleosts, where the ovary lumen has continuity with the oviduct.<sup about="#mwt501" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Guimaraes"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Guimaraes-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 61;" id="mwA6g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA6k">[61]</span></a></sup> Secondary gymnovaries are found in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Salmonid" title="Salmonid" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA6o">salmonids</a> and a few other teleosts.</p>

<p id="mwA6s"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oogonia" title="Oogonia" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA6w">Oogonia</a> development in teleosts fish varies according to the group, and the determination of oogenesis dynamics allows the understanding of maturation and fertilization processes. Changes in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus" id="mwA60">nucleus</a>, ooplasm, and the surrounding layers characterize the oocyte maturation process.<sup about="#mwt503" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Guimaraes"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Guimaraes-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 61;" id="mwA64"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA68">[61]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA7A">Postovulatory <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovarian_follicle" title="Ovarian follicle" id="mwA7E">follicles</a> are structures formed after oocyte release; they do not have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Endocrine" title="Endocrine" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA7I">endocrine</a> function, present a wide irregular lumen, and are rapidly reabsorbed in a process involving the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis" id="mwA7M">apoptosis</a> of follicular cells. A degenerative process called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Follicular_atresia" title="Follicular atresia" id="mwA7Q">follicular atresia</a> reabsorbs vitellogenic oocytes not spawned. This process can also occur, but less frequently, in oocytes in other development stages.<sup about="#mwt505" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Guimaraes_61-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Guimaraes"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Guimaraes-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 61;" id="mwA7U"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA7Y">[61]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA7c">Some fish, like the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./California_sheephead" title="California sheephead" id="mwA7g">California sheephead</a>, are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite" id="mwA7k">hermaphrodites</a>, having both testes and ovaries either at different phases in their life cycle or, as in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hamlet_(fish)" title="Hamlet (fish)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA7o">hamlets</a>, have them simultaneously.</p>

<p id="mwA7s">Over 97% of all known fish are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oviparous" title="Oviparous" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA7w">oviparous</a>,<sup about="#mwt510" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Scott_63-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Scott"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Scott-63"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Scott-63" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 63;" id="mwA70"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA74">[63]</span></a></sup> that is, the eggs develop outside the mother's body. Examples of oviparous fish include <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Salmon" title="Salmon" id="mwA78">salmon</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Goldfish" title="Goldfish" id="mwA8A">goldfish</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cichlid" title="Cichlid" id="mwA8E">cichlids</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tuna" title="Tuna" id="mwA8I">tuna</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Eel" title="Eel" id="mwA8M">eels</a>. In the majority of these species, fertilisation takes place outside the mother's body, with the male and female fish shedding their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gamete" title="Gamete" id="mwA8Q">gametes</a> into the surrounding water. However, a few oviparous fish practice internal fertilization, with the male using some sort of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ichthyology_terms#I" title="Ichthyology terms" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA8U">intromittent organ</a> to deliver sperm into the genital opening of the female, most notably the oviparous sharks, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Horn_shark" title="Horn shark" id="mwA8Y">horn shark</a>, and oviparous rays, such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skate_(fish)" title="Skate (fish)" id="mwA8c">skates</a>. In these cases, the male is equipped with a pair of modified <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pelvic_fin" title="Pelvic fin" id="mwA8g">pelvic fins</a> known as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Clasper" title="Clasper" id="mwA8k">claspers</a>.</p>

<p id="mwA8o">Marine fish can produce high numbers of eggs which are often released into the open water column. The eggs have an average diameter of <span about="#mwt511" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"1"},"2":{"wt":"mm"},"sigfig":{"wt":"1"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwA8s">1 millimetre (0.04</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt511"> </span><span about="#mwt511">in)</span>.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" typeof="mw:Extension/gallery" about="#mwt513" data-mw='{"name":"gallery","attrs":{"widths":"90","heights":"150"},"body":{"extsrc":"\nFile:Oeufs002b,57.png|Egg of [[lamprey]]\nFile:Oeufs002b,54.png|Egg of [[catshark]] ([[mermaids&apos; purse]])\nFile:Oeufs002b,55.png|Egg of [[bullhead shark]]\nFile:Oeufs002b,56.png|Egg of [[chimaera]]\n"}}' id="mwA8w">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 125px;" id="mwA80"><div class="thumb" style="width: 120px; height: 180px;" id="mwA84"><span typeof="mw:File" id="mwA88"><a href="./File:Oeufs002b,57.png" class="mw-file-description" title="Egg of lamprey" id="mwA9A"><img alt="Egg of lamprey" resource="./File:Oeufs002b,57.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Oeufs002b%2C57.png/45px-Oeufs002b%2C57.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="2094" data-file-type="bitmap" height="150" width="45" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Oeufs002b%2C57.png/67px-Oeufs002b%2C57.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Oeufs002b%2C57.png/90px-Oeufs002b%2C57.png 2x" id="mwA9E"/></a></span></div><div class="gallerytext" id="mwA9I">Egg of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lamprey" title="Lamprey" id="mwA9M">lamprey</a></div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 125px;" id="mwA9Q"><div class="thumb" style="width: 120px; height: 180px;" id="mwA9U"><span typeof="mw:File" id="mwA9Y"><a href="./File:Oeufs002b,54.png" class="mw-file-description" title="Egg of catshark (mermaids' purse)" id="mwA9c"><img alt="Egg of catshark (mermaids' purse)" resource="./File:Oeufs002b,54.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Oeufs002b%2C54.png/52px-Oeufs002b%2C54.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="804" data-file-height="2304" data-file-type="bitmap" height="150" width="52" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Oeufs002b%2C54.png/78px-Oeufs002b%2C54.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Oeufs002b%2C54.png/104px-Oeufs002b%2C54.png 2x" id="mwA9g"/></a></span></div><div class="gallerytext" id="mwA9k">Egg of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Catshark" title="Catshark" id="mwA9o">catshark</a> (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mermaids'_purse" title="Mermaids' purse" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA9s">mermaids' purse</a>)</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 125px;" id="mwA9w"><div class="thumb" style="width: 120px; height: 180px;" id="mwA90"><span typeof="mw:File" id="mwA94"><a href="./File:Oeufs002b,55.png" class="mw-file-description" title="Egg of bullhead shark" id="mwA98"><img alt="Egg of bullhead shark" resource="./File:Oeufs002b,55.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Oeufs002b%2C55.png/80px-Oeufs002b%2C55.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="906" data-file-height="1695" data-file-type="bitmap" height="150" width="80" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Oeufs002b%2C55.png/120px-Oeufs002b%2C55.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Oeufs002b%2C55.png/160px-Oeufs002b%2C55.png 2x" id="mwA-A"/></a></span></div><div class="gallerytext" id="mwA-E">Egg of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bullhead_shark" title="Bullhead shark" id="mwA-I">bullhead shark</a></div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 125px;" id="mwA-M"><div class="thumb" style="width: 120px; height: 180px;" id="mwA-Q"><span typeof="mw:File" id="mwA-U"><a href="./File:Oeufs002b,56.png" class="mw-file-description" title="Egg of chimaera" id="mwA-Y"><img alt="Egg of chimaera" resource="./File:Oeufs002b,56.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Oeufs002b%2C56.png/38px-Oeufs002b%2C56.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="684" data-file-height="2708" data-file-type="bitmap" height="150" width="38" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Oeufs002b%2C56.png/56px-Oeufs002b%2C56.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Oeufs002b%2C56.png/75px-Oeufs002b%2C56.png 2x" id="mwA-c"/></a></span></div><div class="gallerytext" id="mwA-g">Egg of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chimaera" title="Chimaera" id="mwA-k">chimaera</a></div></li>
</ul>

<!-- Deleted image removed: [[File:Fishegg A.Slotwinski TAFI.jpg|thumb|An example of [[zooplankton]]|alt=Photo of semi&#x2D;transparent creature with a darker, yolk&#x2D;like central structure and other approximately round internal elements]] -->
<p id="mwA-o">The newly hatched young of oviparous fish are called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ichthyoplankton" title="Ichthyoplankton" id="mwA-s">larvae</a>. They are usually poorly formed, carry a large <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Yolk_sac" title="Yolk sac" id="mwA-w">yolk sac</a> (for nourishment), and are very different in appearance from juvenile and adult specimens. The larval period in oviparous fish is relatively short (usually only several weeks), and larvae rapidly grow and change appearance and structure (a process termed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis" id="mwA-0">metamorphosis</a>) to become juveniles. During this transition larvae must switch from their yolk sac to feeding on <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton" id="mwA-4">zooplankton</a> prey, a process which depends on typically inadequate zooplankton density, starving many larvae.</p>

<p id="mwA-8">In <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovoviviparous" title="Ovoviviparous" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA_A">ovoviviparous</a> fish the eggs develop inside the mother's body after internal fertilization but receive little or no nourishment directly from the mother, depending instead on the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Yolk" title="Yolk" id="mwA_E">yolk</a>. Each embryo develops in its own egg. Familiar examples of ovoviviparous fish include <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Guppy" title="Guppy" id="mwA_I">guppies</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Angel_shark" title="Angel shark" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA_M">angel sharks</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth" id="mwA_Q">coelacanths</a>.</p>

<p id="mwA_U">Some species of fish are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Viviparous" title="Viviparous" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA_Y">viviparous</a>. In such species the mother retains the eggs and nourishes the embryos. Typically, viviparous fish have a structure analogous to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placenta" title="Placenta" id="mwA_c">placenta</a> seen in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placental_mammals" title="Placental mammals" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA_g">mammals</a> connecting the mother's blood supply with that of the embryo. Examples of viviparous fish include the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Embiotocidae" title="Embiotocidae" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA_k">surf-perches</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Goodeidae" title="Goodeidae" id="mwA_o">splitfins</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lemon_shark" title="Lemon shark" id="mwA_s">lemon shark</a>. Some viviparous fish exhibit <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oophagy" title="Oophagy" id="mwA_w">oophagy</a>, in which the developing embryos eat other eggs produced by the mother. This has been observed primarily among sharks, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shortfin_mako" title="Shortfin mako" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA_0">shortfin mako</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Porbeagle" title="Porbeagle" id="mwA_4">porbeagle</a>, but is known for a few bony fish as well, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Halfbeak" title="Halfbeak" id="mwA_8">halfbeak</a> <i id="mwBAA">Nomorhamphus ebrardtii</i>.<sup about="#mwt516" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Meisner_64-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Meisner"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Meisner-64"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Meisner-64" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 64;" id="mwBAE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBAI">[64]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Intrauterine_cannibalism" title="Intrauterine cannibalism" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBAM">Intrauterine cannibalism</a> is an even more unusual mode of vivipary, in which the largest embryos eat weaker and smaller siblings. This behavior is also most commonly found among sharks, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Grey_nurse_shark" title="Grey nurse shark" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBAQ">grey nurse shark</a>, but has also been reported for <i id="mwBAU">Nomorhamphus ebrardtii</i>.<sup about="#mwt517" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Meisner_64-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Meisner"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Meisner-64" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 64;" id="mwBAY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBAc">[64]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBAg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fishkeeping" title="Fishkeeping" id="mwBAk">Aquarists</a> commonly refer to ovoviviparous and viviparous fish as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Livebearer" title="Livebearer" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBAo">livebearers</a>.</p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="25" id="mwBAs"><h2 id="Acoustic_communication">Acoustic communication</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt518" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"see also","href":"./Template:See_also"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Acoustic communication in aquatic animals"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBAw"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt518" id="mwBA0">See also: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Acoustic_communication_in_aquatic_animals" title="Acoustic communication in aquatic animals" class="mw-redirect">Acoustic communication in aquatic animals</a></div>

<p id="mwBA4">Acoustic communication in fish involves the transmission of acoustic signals from one individual of a species to another. The production of sounds as a means of communication among fish is most often used in the context of feeding, aggression or courtship behaviour.<sup about="#mwt523" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Bioacoustics_3-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Bioacoustics"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Bioacoustics-3" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 3;" id="mwBA8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBBA">[3]</span></a></sup> The sounds emitted can vary depending on the species and stimulus involved. Fish can produce either stridulatory sounds by moving components of the skeletal system, or can produce non-stridulatory sounds by manipulating specialized organs such as the swimbladder.<sup about="#mwt527" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Journal"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Journal-65"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Journal-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwBBE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBBI">[65]</span></a></sup></p>

<section data-mw-section-id="26" id="mwBBM"><h3 id="Stridulatory">Stridulatory</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBBQ"><a href="./File:French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBBU"><img resource="./File:French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg/220px-French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" data-file-type="bitmap" height="147" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg/330px-French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg/440px-French_grunts_-_Haemulon_flavolineatum.jpg 2x" id="mwBBY"/></a><figcaption id="mwBBc">French grunts – <i id="mwBBg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Haemulon_flavolineatum" title="Haemulon flavolineatum" id="mwBBk">Haemulon flavolineatum</a></i></figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwBBo">There are some species of fish that can produce sounds by rubbing or grinding their bones together. These noises produced by bone-on-bone interactions are known as 'stridulatory sounds'.<sup about="#mwt529" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Journal"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Journal-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwBBs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBBw">[65]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBB0">An example of this is seen in <i id="mwBB4"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Haemulon_flavolineatum" title="Haemulon flavolineatum" id="mwBB8">Haemulon flavolineatum</a></i>, a species commonly referred to as the 'French grunt fish', as it produces a grunting noise by grinding its teeth together.<sup about="#mwt534" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Journal"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Journal-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwBCA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCE">[65]</span></a></sup> This behaviour is most pronounced when the <i id="mwBCI">H. flavolineatum</i> is in distress situations.<sup about="#mwt535" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Journal"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Journal-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwBCM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCQ">[65]</span></a></sup> The grunts produced by this species of fishes generate a frequency of approximately 700<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBCU"> </span>Hz, and last approximately 47 milliseconds.<sup about="#mwt536" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Journal"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Journal-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwBCY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCc">[65]</span></a></sup> The <i id="mwBCg">H. flavolineatum</i> does not emit sounds with frequencies greater than 1000<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBCk"> </span>Hz, and does not detect sounds that have frequencies greater than 1050<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBCo"> </span>Hz.<sup about="#mwt537" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_65-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Journal"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Journal-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwBCs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCw">[65]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBC0">In a study conducted by Oliveira et al. (2014), the longsnout seahorse, <i id="mwBC4"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hippocampus_reidi" title="Hippocampus reidi" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBC8">Hippocampus reidi</a></i>, was recorded producing two different categories of sounds; 'clicks' and 'growls'. The sounds emitted by the <i id="mwBDA">H. reidi</i> are accomplished by rubbing their coronet bone across the grooved section of their neurocranium.<sup about="#mwt546" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-66" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-66"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-66" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 66;" id="mwBDE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBDI">[66]</span></a></sup> 'Clicking' sounds were found to be primarily produced during courtship and feeding, and the frequencies of clicks were within the range of 50<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBDM"> </span>Hz-800<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBDQ"> </span>Hz.<sup about="#mwt550" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Zoology"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Zoology-67"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Zoology-67" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 67;" id="mwBDU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBDY">[67]</span></a></sup> The frequencies were noted to be on the higher end of the range during spawning periods, when the female and male fishes were less than fifteen centimeters apart.<sup about="#mwt551" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Zoology"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Zoology-67" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 67;" id="mwBDc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBDg">[67]</span></a></sup> Growl sounds were produced when the <i id="mwBDk">H. reidi</i> encountered stressful situations, such as handling by researchers.<sup about="#mwt552" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Zoology"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Zoology-67" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 67;" id="mwBDo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBDs">[67]</span></a></sup> The 'growl' sounds consist of a series of sound pulses and are emitted simultaneously with body vibrations.<sup about="#mwt553" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Zoology_67-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Zoology"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Zoology-67" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 67;" id="mwBDw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBD0">[67]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="27" id="mwBD4"><h3 id="Non-stridulatory">Non-stridulatory</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBD8"><a href="./File:C(flipped).jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBEA"><img resource="./File:C(flipped).jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/C%28flipped%29.jpg/220px-C%28flipped%29.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1532" data-file-type="bitmap" height="187" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/C%28flipped%29.jpg/330px-C%28flipped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/C%28flipped%29.jpg/440px-C%28flipped%29.jpg 2x" id="mwBEE"/></a><figcaption id="mwBEI"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;" about="#mwt554" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"center","href":"./Template:Center"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Oyster toadfish"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBEM">Oyster toadfish</div></figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwBEQ">Some fish species create noise by engaging specialized muscles that contract and cause swimbladder vibrations.</p>

<p id="mwBEU"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oyster_toadfish" title="Oyster toadfish" id="mwBEY">Oyster toadfish</a> produce loud grunting sounds by contracting muscles located along the sides of their swim bladder, known as sonic muscles <sup about="#mwt565" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_68-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Experimental"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Experimental-68"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Experimental-68" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 68;" id="mwBEc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBEg">[68]</span></a></sup> Female and male toadfishes emit short-duration grunts, often as a fright response.<sup about="#mwt569" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-69" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-69"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-69" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 69;" id="mwBEk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBEo">[69]</span></a></sup> In addition to short-duration grunts, male toadfishes produce "boat whistle calls".<sup about="#mwt570" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-plos_70-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"plos"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-plos-70" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 70;" id="mwBEs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBEw">[70]</span></a></sup> These calls are longer in duration, lower in frequency, and are primarily used to attract mates.<sup about="#mwt571" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-plos_70-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"plos"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-plos-70" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 70;" id="mwBE0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBE4">[70]</span></a></sup> The sounds emitted by the <i id="mwBE8">O. tao</i> have frequency range of 140<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBFA"> </span>Hz to 260<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBFE"> </span>Hz.<sup about="#mwt575" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-plos_70-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"plos"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-plos-70"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-plos-70" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 70;" id="mwBFI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFM">[70]</span></a></sup> The frequencies of the calls depend on the rate at which the sonic muscles contract.<sup about="#mwt579" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-71" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-71"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-71" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 71;" id="mwBFQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFU">[71]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt580" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_68-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Experimental"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Experimental-68" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 68;" id="mwBFY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFc">[68]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBFg">The red drum, <i id="mwBFk"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sciaenops_ocellatus" title="Sciaenops ocellatus" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBFo">Sciaenops ocellatus</a></i>, produces drumming sounds by vibrating its swimbladder.<sup about="#mwt590" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Elsevier"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Elsevier-72"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Elsevier-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwBFs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFw">[72]</span></a></sup> Vibrations are caused by the rapid contraction of sonic muscles that surround the dorsal aspect of the swimbladder.<sup about="#mwt591" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Elsevier"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Elsevier-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwBF0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBF4">[72]</span></a></sup> These vibrations result in repeated sounds with frequencies that range from 100 to >200<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBF8"> </span>Hz.<sup about="#mwt592" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Elsevier"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Elsevier-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwBGA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBGE">[72]</span></a></sup> The <i id="mwBGI">S. ocellatus</i> can produce different calls depending on the stimuli involved.<sup about="#mwt593" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Elsevier"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Elsevier-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwBGM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBGQ">[72]</span></a></sup> The sounds created in courtship situations are different from those made during distressing events such as predatorial attacks.<sup about="#mwt594" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Elsevier"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Elsevier-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwBGU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBGY">[72]</span></a></sup> Unlike the males of the <i id="mwBGc">S. ocellatus</i> species, the females of this species do not produce sounds and lack sound-producing (sonic) muscles.<sup about="#mwt595" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Elsevier_72-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Elsevier"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-Elsevier-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwBGg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBGk">[72]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="28" id="mwBGo"><h2 id="Diseases">Diseases</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt596" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Fish diseases and parasites"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBGs"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt596" id="mwBGw">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fish_diseases_and_parasites" title="Fish diseases and parasites" class="mw-redirect">Fish diseases and parasites</a></div>
<p id="mwBG0">Like other animals, fish suffer from diseases and parasites. To prevent disease they have a variety of defenses. <i id="mwBG4">Non-specific</i> defenses include the skin and scales, as well as the mucus layer secreted by the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Epidermis_(skin)" title="Epidermis (skin)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBG8">epidermis</a> that traps and inhibits the growth of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Microorganism" title="Microorganism" id="mwBHA">microorganisms</a>. If <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pathogen" title="Pathogen" id="mwBHE">pathogens</a> breach these defenses, fish can develop an <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Inflammation" title="Inflammation" id="mwBHI">inflammatory response</a> that increases blood flow to the infected region and delivers <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./White_blood_cells" title="White blood cells" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBHM">white blood cells</a> that attempt to destroy pathogens. Specific defenses respond to particular pathogens recognised by the fish's body, i.e., an <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Immune_response" title="Immune response" id="mwBHQ">immune response</a>.<sup about="#mwt601" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199795–96_73-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"pp":{"wt":"95–96"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey199795–96-73" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 73;" id="mwBHU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBHY">[73]</span></a></sup> In recent years, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vaccine" title="Vaccine" id="mwBHc">vaccines</a> have become widely used in aquaculture and also with ornamental fish, for example <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Furunculosis" title="Furunculosis" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBHg">furunculosis</a> vaccines in farmed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Salmon" title="Salmon" id="mwBHk">salmon</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Koi_herpes_virus" title="Koi herpes virus" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBHo">koi herpes virus</a> in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Koi" title="Koi" id="mwBHs">koi</a>.<sup about="#mwt607" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-74" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-74"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-74" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 74;" id="mwBHw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBH0">[74]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt611" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-75" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-75"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-75" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 75;" id="mwBH4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBH8">[75]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBIA">Some species use <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cleaner_fish" title="Cleaner fish" id="mwBIE">cleaner fish</a> to remove external parasites. The best known of these are the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bluestreak_cleaner_wrasse" title="Bluestreak cleaner wrasse" id="mwBII">bluestreak cleaner wrasses</a> of the genus <i id="mwBIM">Labroides</i> found on <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coral_reef" title="Coral reef" id="mwBIQ">coral reefs</a> in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean" id="mwBIU">Indian</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean" id="mwBIY">Pacific</a> oceans. These small fish maintain so-called "cleaning stations" where other fish congregate and perform specific movements to attract the attention of the cleaners.<sup about="#mwt613" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997380_76-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Helfman"},"2":{"wt":"Collette"},"3":{"wt":"Facey"},"4":{"wt":"1997"},"p":{"wt":"380"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelfmanColletteFacey1997380-76" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 76;" id="mwBIc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBIg">[76]</span></a></sup> Cleaning behaviors have been observed in a number of fish groups, including an interesting case between two cichlids of the same genus, <i id="mwBIk"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Orange_chromide" title="Orange chromide" id="mwBIo">Etroplus maculatus</a></i>, the cleaner, and the much larger <i id="mwBIs">Etroplus suratensis</i>.<sup about="#mwt619" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-77" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-77"}}'><a href="./Fish#cite_note-77" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 77;" id="mwBIw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBI0">[77]</span></a></sup></p>

<section data-mw-section-id="29" id="mwBI4"><h3 id="Immune_system">Immune system</h3>
